id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Aircraft carrier</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercarrier&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Supercarrier">Supercarrier</a>)</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Warship that serves as a seagoing airbase</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fleet_5_nations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fleet_5_nations.jpg/300px-Fleet_5_nations.jpg" width="300" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fleet_5_nations.jpg/450px-Fleet_5_nations.jpg 1.5x, class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fleet_5_nations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four modern aircraft carriers of various types\xe2\x80\x94<a Stennis</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/French_aircraft_carrier_Charles_de_Gaulle_(R91)" class="mw-redirect" title="French aircraft carrier Charles de Gaulle (R91)"><i>Charles de Gaulle</i> (French Navy)</a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_John_F._Kennedy_(CV-67)" title="USS John F. Kennedy (CV-67)">USS&#160;<i>John F. Kennedy</i></a>, helicopter carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ocean_(L12)" title="HMS Ocean (L12)">HMS&#160;<i>Ocean</i></a>\xe2\x80\x94and escort vessels, 2002</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg/280px-Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg" width="280" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg/420px-Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg/560px-Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="804" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Principe-de-Asturias_Wasp_Forrestal_Invincible_1991_DN-ST-92-01129s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>From bottom to top:</i> Spanish light <a href="/wiki/V/STOL" title="V/STOL">V/STOL</a> carrier <a href="/wiki/Spanish_aircraft_carrier_Pr%C3%ADncipe_de_Asturias" title="Spanish aircraft carrier Pr\xc3\xadncipe de Asturias"><i>Pr\xc3\xadncipe de Asturias</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">amphibious assault ship</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Wasp_(LHD-1)" title="USS Wasp (LHD-1)">USS&#160;<i>Wasp</i></a>, fleet carrier <a href="/wiki/USS_Forrestal_(CV-59)" title="USS Forrestal (CV-59)">USS&#160;<i>Forrestal</i></a>, and light V/STOL carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Invincible_(R05)" title="HMS Invincible (R05)">HMS&#160;<i>Invincible</i></a>, showing size differences of late 20th century carriers, 1991</div></div></div>\n<p>An <b>aircraft carrier</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Warship" title="Warship">warship</a> that serves as a seagoing <a href="/wiki/Airbase" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbase">airbase</a>, equipped with a full-length <a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">flight deck</a> and facilities for <a href="/wiki/Carrier-based_aircraft" title="Carrier-based aircraft">carrying, arming, deploying, and recovering aircraft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Typically, it is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">capital ship</a> of a fleet, as it allows a naval force to <a href="/wiki/Power_projection" title="Power projection">project</a> <a href="/wiki/Air_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Air power">air power</a> worldwide without depending on <a href="/wiki/Staging_area" title="Staging area">local bases for staging aircraft operations</a>. Carriers have evolved since their inception in the early twentieth century from wooden vessels used to deploy <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloons</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear-powered</a> warships that carry numerous <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strike_aircraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Strike aircraft">strike aircraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_helicopter" title="Military helicopter">helicopters</a>, and other types of aircraft. While heavier aircraft such as fixed-wing <a href="/wiki/Gunship" title="Gunship">gunships</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bomber" title="Bomber">bombers</a> have been launched from aircraft carriers, it is currently not possible to land them. By its diplomatic and tactical power, its mobility, its autonomy and the variety of its means, the aircraft carrier is often the centerpiece of modern combat fleets. Tactically or even strategically, it replaced the <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleship</a> in the role of flagship of a fleet. One of its great advantages is that, by sailing in international waters, it does not interfere with any territorial sovereignty and thus obviates the need for overflight authorizations from third party countries, reduce the times and transit distances of aircraft and therefore significantly increase the time of availability on the combat zone.\n</p><p>There is no single definition of an "aircraft carrier",<sup id="cite_ref-gscarrier_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gscarrier-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and modern navies use several variants of the type. These variants are sometimes categorized as sub-types of aircraft carriers,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and sometimes as distinct types of <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">naval aviation</a>-capable ships.<sup id="cite_ref-gscarrier_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gscarrier-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Aircraft carriers may be classified according to the type of aircraft they carry and their operational assignments. <a href="/wiki/Admiral_(Royal_Navy)" title="Admiral (Royal Navy)">Admiral</a> Sir <a href="/wiki/Mark_Stanhope" title="Mark Stanhope">Mark Stanhope</a>, RN, former <a href="/wiki/First_Sea_Lord" title="First Sea Lord">First Sea Lord (head)</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, has said, "To put it simply, countries that aspire to strategic international influence have aircraft carriers."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>, while <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a>, also said: "An aircraft carrier is 100,000 tons of diplomacy".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of September 2018, there are 41 active aircraft carriers in the world operated by thirteen navies. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> has 11 large nuclear-powered <a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">fleet carriers</a>\xe2\x80\x94carrying around 80 fighter jets each\xe2\x80\x94the largest carriers in the world; the total combined deckspace is over twice that of all other nations combined.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As well as the aircraft carrier fleet, the U.S. Navy has nine <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">amphibious assault ships</a> used primarily for helicopters, although these also carry up to 20 vertical or short take-off and landing (<a href="/wiki/V/STOL" title="V/STOL">V/STOL</a>) fighter jets and are similar in size to medium-sized fleet carriers. China, France, India, Russia, and the UK each operate a single large/medium-size carrier, with capacity from 30 to 60 fighter jets. Italy operates two light fleet carriers and Spain operates one. <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_carriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Helicopter carriers">Helicopter carriers</a> are operated by Japan (4), France (3), Australia (2), Egypt (2), Brazil (1), South Korea (1), and Thailand (1). Future aircraft carriers are under construction or in planning by Brazil, China, India, Russia, the United Kingdom, and the United States.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" carriers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-57"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_carrier">Types of carrier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class</a> aircraft carrier of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_types">Basic types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Basic types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">Amphibious assault ship</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare_carrier" title="Anti-submarine warfare carrier">Anti-submarine warfare carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Balloon_carrier" title="Balloon carrier">Balloon carrier</a> and balloon tenders</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Escort_carrier" title="Escort carrier">Escort carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">Fleet carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Flight_deck_cruiser" title="Flight deck cruiser">Flight deck cruiser</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Helicopter_carrier" title="Helicopter carrier">Helicopter carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Light_aircraft_carrier" title="Light aircraft carrier">Light aircraft carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sea_Control_Ship" title="Sea Control Ship">Sea Control Ship</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Seaplane_tender" title="Seaplane tender">Seaplane tender</a> and seaplane carriers</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_cruiser" title="Aircraft cruiser">Aircraft cruiser</a></li></ul>\n<p><small><i>(note: some of the types listed here are not strictly defined as aircraft carriers by some sources)</i></small>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_role">By role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: By role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A <i><a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">fleet carrier</a></i> is intended to operate with the main fleet and usually provides an offensive capability. These are the largest carriers capable of fast speeds. By comparison, <i><a href="/wiki/Escort_carrier" title="Escort carrier">escort carriers</a></i> were developed to provide defense for convoys of ships. They were smaller and slower with lower numbers of aircraft carried. Most were built from mercantile hulls or, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Merchant_aircraft_carrier" title="Merchant aircraft carrier">merchant aircraft carriers</a>, were bulk cargo ships with a flight deck added on top. <i><a href="/wiki/Light_aircraft_carrier" title="Light aircraft carrier">Light aircraft carriers</a></i> were fast enough to operate with the main fleet but of smaller size with reduced aircraft capacity. Soviet aircraft carriers now in use by Russia are actually called <i>heavy aviation cruisers</i>; these ships, while sized in the range of large fleet carriers, were designed to deploy alone or with escorts, and provide both strong defensive weaponry and heavy offensive missiles equivalent to a guided missile cruiser, in addition to supporting fighters and helicopters.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_configuration">By configuration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: By configuration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:INS_Vikramaditya_(R33)_close_shot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg/220px-INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg/330px-INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg/440px-INS_Vikramaditya_%28R33%29_close_shot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1476" data-file-height="1034" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:INS_Vikramaditya_(R33)_close_shot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/INS_Vikramaditya" title="INS Vikramaditya">INS&#160;<i>Vikramaditya</i></a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Navy" title="Indian Navy">Indian Navy</a> has the <a href="/wiki/STOBAR" title="STOBAR">STOBAR</a> configuration.</div></div></div>\n<p>Aircraft carriers today are usually divided into the following four categories based on the way that aircraft take off and land:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Catapult-assisted take-off barrier arrested-recovery</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/CATOBAR" title="CATOBAR">CATOBAR</a></i>): these carriers generally carry the largest, heaviest, and most heavily armed aircraft, although smaller CATOBAR carriers may have other limitations (weight capacity of aircraft elevator, etc.). All CATOBAR carriers in service today are nuclear powered. Two nations currently operate carriers of this type: ten <i>Nimitz</i> class and one <i>Gerald R. Ford</i> class fleet carriers by the United States, and one medium-sized carrier by France, for a world total of twelve in service.</li>\n<li><i>Short take-off but arrested-recovery</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/STOBAR" title="STOBAR">STOBAR</a></i>): these carriers are generally limited to carrying lighter fixed-wing aircraft with more limited payloads. STOBAR carrier air wings, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-33" title="Sukhoi Su-33">Sukhoi Su-33</a> and future <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan_MiG-29K" title="Mikoyan MiG-29K">Mikoyan MiG-29K</a> wings of <a href="/wiki/Russian_aircraft_carrier_Admiral_Kuznetsov" title="Russian aircraft carrier Admiral Kuznetsov"><i>Admiral Kuznetsov</i></a> are often geared primarily towards air superiority and fleet defense roles rather than strike/power projection tasks,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which require heavier payloads (bombs and air-to-ground missiles). Today China, India, and Russia each operate one carrier of this type \xe2\x80\x93 a total of three in service currently.</li>\n<li><i>Short take-off vertical-landing</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/STOVL" title="STOVL">STOVL</a></i>): limited to carrying STOVL aircraft. STOVL aircraft, such as the <a href="/wiki/Harrier_Jump_Jet" title="Harrier Jump Jet">Harrier Jump Jet</a> family and <a href="/wiki/Yakovlev_Yak-38" title="Yakovlev Yak-38">Yakovlev Yak-38</a> generally have limited payloads, lower performance, and high fuel consumption when compared with conventional fixed-wing aircraft; however, a new generation of STOVL aircraft, currently consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35</a>B, has much improved performance. The US has nine STOVL amphibious assault ships. The UK has a class of two 65,000 ton<sup id="cite_ref-HMSQE_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HMSQE-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> STOVL aircraft carriers, <a href="/wiki/List_of_aircraft_carriers_of_the_Royal_Navy" title="List of aircraft carriers of the Royal Navy">HMS <i>Queen Elizabeth</i> and HMS <i>Prince of Wales</i></a>, the largest warships ever built for the Royal Navy; with one in service and the other being fitted out. Italy operates two in the light fleet role, and Spain operates one amphibious assault ship as a STOVL aircraft carrier, giving a total of thirteen STOVL carriers in active service; (Thailand has one active STOVL carrier but she no longer has any operational STOVL aircraft in inventory so is used and counted as a helicopter carrier).</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Helicopter_carrier" title="Helicopter carrier">Helicopter carrier</a></i>: Helicopter carriers have a similar appearance to other aircraft carriers but operate only helicopters \xe2\x80\x93 those that mainly operate helicopters but can also operate fixed-wing aircraft are known as STOVL carriers (see above). There are currently fourteen helicopter carriers (that solely operate helicopters and not fixed-wing aircraft), operated by seven navies, in commission today. Japan has four of this type, France three, Australia two, Egypt two, and South Korea, Thailand, and Brazil have one each. In the past, some conventional carriers were converted and called <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">commando carriers</a> by the Royal Navy. Some helicopter carriers, but not all, are classified as <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ships" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphibious assault ships">amphibious assault ships</a>, tasked with landing and supporting ground forces on enemy territory.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_size">By size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: By size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg/220px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg/330px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg/440px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3839" data-file-height="1967" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Japanese carrier <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Shinano" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Shinano"><i>Shinano</i></a> was the biggest carrier in World War II, and the largest ship destroyed by a submarine.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">Fleet carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Light_aircraft_carrier" title="Light aircraft carrier">Light aircraft carrier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Escort_carrier" title="Escort carrier">Escort carrier</a></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercarrier">Supercarrier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Supercarrier">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the fictional drama television series, see <a href="/wiki/Supercarrier_(TV_series)" title="Supercarrier (TV series)">Supercarrier (TV series)</a>.</div>\n<p>The appellation "supercarrier" is not an official designation with any national navy, but a term used predominantly by the media and typically when reporting on new and upcoming aircraft carrier types. It is also used when comparing carriers of various sizes and capabilities, both current and past. It was first used by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in 1938,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> in an article about the Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(91)" title="HMS Ark Royal (91)">HMS&#160;<i>Ark Royal</i></a>, that had a length of 209 metres (686&#160;ft), a displacement of 22,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> and was designed to carry 72 aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Since then, aircraft carriers have consistently grown in size, both in length and displacement, as well as improved capabilities; in defense, sensors, electronic warfare, propulsion, range, launch and recovery systems, number and types of aircraft carried and number of sorties flown per day. \n</p><p>While the current classes in service, or planned, with the navies of China, India, Russia, and the UK, with displacements ranging from 65,000<sup id="cite_ref-HMSQE_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HMSQE-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> to 85,000 tonnes,<sup id="cite_ref-China_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> lengths ranging from 280 meters (920 ft)<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> to 320 meters (1,050 ft)<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and varying capabilities, have been described as "supercarriers",<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-China_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> the largest "supercarriers" currently in service are with the US Navy,<sup id="cite_ref-USA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> with displacements exceeding 100,000 tonnes,<sup id="cite_ref-USA_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> lengths of over 337 meters (1,106 ft),<sup id="cite_ref-USA_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and capabilities that match or exceed that of any other class.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hull_type_identification_symbols">Hull type identification symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hull type identification symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Several systems of identification symbol for aircraft carriers and related types of ship have been used. These include the <a href="/wiki/Pennant_number" title="Pennant number">pennant numbers</a> used by the Royal Navy and some <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> countries, the <a href="/wiki/Hull_classification_symbol" title="Hull classification symbol">hull classification symbols</a> used by the US, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and some other countries,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hull_classification_symbol_(Canada)" title="Hull classification symbol (Canada)">Canadian hull classification symbols</a>.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>US hull classification symbols for aircraft carriers and related types\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Symbol</th>\n<th>Designation\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CV</td>\n<td>Generic aircraft carrier\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CVA</td>\n<td>Attack carrier\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CVB</td>\n<td>Large aircraft carrier (retired)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CVAN</td>\n<td>Nuclear-powered attack carrier\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CVE</td>\n<td><a Helicopter">Landing Platform Helicopter</a>, a type of amphibious assault ship\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_aircraft_carrier" title="History of the aircraft carrier">History of the aircraft carrier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_for_aircraft_carrier_service" title="Timeline for aircraft carrier service">Timeline for aircraft carrier service</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakamiya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Wakamiya.jpg/220px-Wakamiya.jpg" width="220" height="72" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Wakamiya.jpg/330px-Wakamiya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Wakamiya.jpg/440px-Wakamiya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="775" data-file-height="252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakamiya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Japanese seaplane carrier&#32;<a href="/wiki/Japanese_seaplane_carrier_Wakamiya" title="Japanese seaplane carrier Wakamiya"><i>Wakamiya</i></a> conducted the world\'s first naval-launched air raids in 1914.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">1903 advent</a> of heavier-than-air fixed-wing <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">aircraft</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a>\' first flight at <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Hawk,_North_Carolina" title="Kitty Hawk, North Carolina">Kitty Hawk, North Carolina</a>, was closely followed on 14 November 1910, by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Burton_Ely" title="Eugene Burton Ely">Eugene Burton Ely</a>\'s first experimental take-off of a Curtiss <a href="/wiki/Pusher_configuration" title="Pusher configuration">pusher</a> airplane from the deck of a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> ship, the cruiser <a href="/wiki/USS_Birmingham_(CL-2)" title="USS Birmingham (CL-2)">USS&#160;<i>Birmingham</i></a> anchored off <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Navy_Base" class="mw-redirect" title="Norfolk Navy Base">Norfolk Navy Base</a> in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. Two months later, on 18 January 1911, Ely landed his Curtiss pusher airplane on a platform on the <a href="/wiki/Armored_cruiser" title="Armored cruiser">armored cruiser</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Pennsylvania_(ACR-4)" title="USS Pennsylvania (ACR-4)">USS <i>Pennsylvania</i></a> anchored in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay" title="San Francisco Bay">San Francisco Bay</a>. On 9 May 1912, the first airplane take-off from a ship underway was made from the deck of the Royal Navy\'s pre-dreadnaught battleship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hibernia_(1905)" title="HMS Hibernia (1905)">HMS&#160;<i>Hibernia</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seaplane_tender" title="Seaplane tender">Seaplane tender</a> support ships came next, with the French <a href="/wiki/French_seaplane_carrier_Foudre" title="French seaplane carrier Foudre"><i>Foudre</i></a> of 1911. Early in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> ship <a href="/wiki/Japanese_seaplane_carrier_Wakamiya" title="Japanese seaplane carrier Wakamiya"><i>Wakamiya</i></a> conducted the world\'s first successful ship-launched air raid:<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> on 6 September 1914, a Farman aircraft launched by <i>Wakamiya</i> attacked the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Navy" title="Austro-Hungarian Navy">Austro-Hungarian</a> cruiser <a href="/wiki/SMS_Kaiserin_Elisabeth" title="SMS Kaiserin Elisabeth">SMS&#160;<i>Kaiserin Elisabeth</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_German_Navy" title="Imperial German Navy">Imperial German</a> gunboat <i>Jaguar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhou_Bay" title="Jiaozhou Bay">Kiaochow Bay</a> off <a href="/wiki/Tsingtao" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsingtao">Tsingtao</a>; neither was hit.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The first carrier-launched airstrike was the <a href="/wiki/Tondern_Raid" class="mw-redirect" title="Tondern Raid">Tondern Raid</a> in July 1918. Seven <a href="/wiki/Sopwith_Camels" class="mw-redirect" title="Sopwith Camels">Sopwith Camels</a> launched from the converted battlecruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Furious_(47)" title="HMS Furious (47)">HMS&#160;<i>Furious</i>&#160;(47)</a> damaged the German airbase at Tondern, Germany (modern day <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B8nder" title="T\xc3\xb8nder">T\xc3\xb8nder</a>, Denmark) and destroyed two <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin" title="Zeppelin">zeppelin airships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg/220px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg/330px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg/440px-Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3482" data-file-height="2264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D_Tokyo_Bay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D" title="Japanese aircraft carrier H\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\x8d"><i>H\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\x8d</i></a> of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> as completed in December 1922.</div></div></div>\n<p>The development of flattop vessels produced the first large fleet ships. In 1918, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Argus_(I49)" title="HMS Argus (I49)">HMS&#160;<i>Argus</i></a> became the world\'s first carrier capable of launching and recovering naval aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Treaty" title="Washington Naval Treaty">Washington Naval Treaty</a> of 1922, which limited the construction of new heavy surface combat ships, most early aircraft carriers were conversions of ships that were laid down (or had served) as different ship types: cargo ships, cruisers, battlecruisers, or battleships. These conversions gave rise to the US <a href="/wiki/Lexington-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Lexington-class aircraft carrier"><i>Lexington</i>-class</a> aircraft carriers (1927), Japanese <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_Akagi" title="Japanese aircraft carrier Akagi"><i>Akagi</i></a>, and British <a href="/wiki/Courageous-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Courageous-class aircraft carrier"><i>Courageous</i>&#x20;class</a>. Specialist carrier evolution was well underway, with several navies ordering and building warships that were purposefully designed to function as aircraft carriers by the mid-1920s. This resulted in the commissioning of ships such as the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aircraft_carrier_H%C5%8Dsh%C5%8D" title="Japanese aircraft carrier H\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\x8d"><i>H\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\x8d</i></a> (1922),<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hermes_(95)" title="HMS Hermes (95)">HMS&#160;<i>Hermes</i></a> (1924, although laid down before <i>H\xc5\x8dsh\xc5\x8d</i> in 1918) and <a href="/wiki/French_aircraft_carrier_B%C3%A9arn" title="French aircraft carrier B\xc3\xa9arn"><i>B\xc3\xa9arn</i></a> (1927). During World War II, these ships would become known as <a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">fleet carriers</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_aircraft_carriers_of_the_Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="List of aircraft carriers of the Second World War">List of aircraft carriers of the Second World War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/220px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/330px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/440px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2130" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(91)" title="HMS Ark Royal (91)">HMS <i>Ark Royal</i></a> in 1939, with Swordfish biplane bombers passing overhead. The British aircraft carrier was involved in the crippling of the German battleship <a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Bismarck" title="German battleship Bismarck"><i>Bismarck</i></a> in May 1941</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CV-6)_in_Puget_Sound,_September_1945.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg/220px-USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg/330px-USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg/440px-USS_Enterprise_%28CV-6%29_in_Puget_Sound%2C_September_1945.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3157" data-file-height="2337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CV-6)_in_Puget_Sound,_September_1945.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CV-6)" title="USS Enterprise (CV-6)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i></a>, the <a href="/wiki/Most_decorated_US_ships_of_World_War_II" title="Most decorated US ships of World War II">most decorated U.S. warship of World War II</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The aircraft carrier dramatically changed naval combat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, because air power was becoming a significant factor in warfare. The advent of aircraft as focal weapons was driven by the superior range, flexibility, and effectiveness of carrier-launched aircraft. They had greater range and precision than naval guns, making them highly effective. The versatility of the carrier was demonstrated in November 1940, when <a href="/wiki/HMS_Illustrious_(87)" title="HMS Illustrious (87)">HMS&#160;<i>Illustrious</i></a> launched a long-range <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Taranto" title="Battle of Taranto">strike on the Italian fleet</a> at their base in <a href="/wiki/Taranto" title="Taranto">Taranto</a>, signalling the beginning of the effective and highly mobile aircraft strikes. This operation in the shallow water harbor incapacitated three of the anchored six battleships at a cost of two torpedo bombers. <a href="/wiki/Asiatic-Pacific_Theater" title="Asiatic-Pacific Theater">World War II in the Pacific Ocean</a> involved clashes between aircraft carrier fleets. The Japanese surprise attack on the American Pacific fleet at <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Pearl_Harbor" title="Naval Station Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> naval / air bases on Sunday, 7 December 1941, was a clear illustration of the power projection capability afforded by a large force of modern carriers. Concentrating six carriers in a single unit turned naval history about, as no other nation had fielded anything comparable. Further versatility was demonstrated during the "<a href="/wiki/Doolittle_Raid" title="Doolittle Raid">Doolittle Raid</a>", on 18 April 1942, when US Navy carrier <a href="/wiki/USS_Hornet_(CV-8)" title="USS Hornet (CV-8)">USS&#160;<i>Hornet</i>&#160;(CV-8)</a> sailed to within 650 nautical miles of Japan and launched 16 <a href="/wiki/North_American_B-25_Mitchell" title="North American B-25 Mitchell">B-25 bombers</a> from her deck in a retaliatory strike on the mainland, including the capital, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>. However, the vulnerability of carriers compared to traditional battleships when forced into a gun-range encounter was quickly illustrated by the sinking of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Glorious" title="HMS Glorious">HMS&#160;<i>Glorious</i></a> by German battleships during the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Campaign" title="Norwegian Campaign">Norwegian campaign in 1940</a>.\n</p><p>This new-found importance of <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">naval aviation</a> forced nations to create a number of carriers, in efforts to provide air superiority cover for every major fleet in order to ward off enemy aircraft. This extensive usage led to the development and construction of \'light\' carriers. <a href="/wiki/Escort_aircraft_carrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Escort aircraft carrier">Escort aircraft carriers</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/USS_Bogue_(CVE-9)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Bogue (CVE-9)">USS&#160;<i>Bogue</i></a>, were sometimes purpose-built but most were converted from merchant ships as a stop-gap measure to provide anti-submarine air support for convoys and amphibious invasions. Following this concept, <a href="/wiki/Light_aircraft_carrier" title="Light aircraft carrier">light aircraft carriers</a> built by the U.S., such as <a href="/wiki/USS_Independence_(CVL-22)" title="USS Independence (CVL-22)">USS&#160;<i>Independence</i></a>, represented a larger, more "militarized" version of the escort carrier. Although with similar complement to escort carriers, they had the advantage of speed from their converted cruiser hulls. The UK <a href="/wiki/1942_Design_Light_Fleet_Carrier" title="1942 Design Light Fleet Carrier">1942 Design Light Fleet Carrier</a> was designed for building quickly by civilian shipyards and with an expected service life of about 3 years.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> They served the Royal Navy during the war, and the hull design was chosen for nearly all aircraft carrier equipped navies after the war, until the 1980s. Emergencies also spurred the creation or conversion of highly unconventional aircraft carriers. <a href="/wiki/CAM_ship" title="CAM ship">CAM ships</a> were cargo-carrying merchant ships that could launch (but not retrieve) a single fighter aircraft from a catapult to defend the convoy from long range land-based German aircraft.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_era">Postwar era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Postwar era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg/220px-USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg/330px-USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg/440px-USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Tripoli_LPH10_a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>USS <i>Tripoli</i>, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> <i>Iwo Jima</i>-class helicopter carrier</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg/220px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg/330px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg/440px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVN-65%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i>&#160;(CVN-65)</a>, "The Big E", the world\'s first nuclear-powered carrier, commissioned in 1961.</div></div></div>\n<p>Before World War II, international naval treaties of <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Treaty" title="Washington Naval Treaty">1922</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Naval_Treaty" title="London Naval Treaty">1930</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Second_London_Naval_Treaty" title="Second London Naval Treaty">1936</a> limited the size of capital ships including carriers. Since World War II, aircraft carrier designs have increased in size to accommodate a steady increase in aircraft size. The large, modern <a href="/wiki/Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Nimitz-class aircraft carrier"><i>Nimitz</i>&#x20;class</a> of U.S.N. carriers has a displacement nearly four times that of the World War II\xe2\x80\x93era <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CV-6)" title="USS Enterprise (CV-6)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i></a>, yet its complement of aircraft is roughly the same\xe2\x80\x94a consequence of the steadily increasing size and weight of individual military aircraft over the years. Today\'s aircraft carriers are so expensive that nations which operate them risk significant political, economic, social and military impact if a carrier is lost, or is even sent to a potential crisis zone or used in conflict.\n</p><p>Modern navies that operate such aircraft carriers treat them as the <a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">capital ship</a> of the fleet, a role previously held by the sailing galleons, frigates and ships-of-the-line and later steam or diesel powered <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleship</a>. This change took place during World War II in response to air power becoming a significant factor in warfare, driven by the superior range, flexibility and effectiveness of carrier-launched aircraft. Following the war, carrier operations continued to increase in size and importance, and along with, carrier designs also increased in size and ability. Some of these larger carriers, dubbed by the media as "supercarriers", displacing 75,000&#160;tonnes or greater, have become the pinnacle of carrier development. Some are powered by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> and form the core of a fleet designed to operate far from home. Amphibious assault ships, such as the <a href="/wiki/Wasp-class_amphibious_assault_ship" title="Wasp-class amphibious assault ship"><i>Wasp</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Mistral-class_amphibious_assault_ship" title="Mistral-class amphibious assault ship"><i>Mistral</i></a> classes, serve the purpose of carrying and landing Marines, and operate a large contingent of helicopters for that purpose. Also known as "commando carriers"<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> or "helicopter carriers", many have the capability to operate <a href="/wiki/VSTOL" class="mw-redirect" title="VSTOL">VSTOL</a> aircraft.\n</p><p>Lacking the firepower of other warships, carriers by themselves are considered vulnerable to attack by other ships, aircraft, submarines, or missiles. Therefore, an aircraft carrier is generally accompanied by a number of other ships to provide protection for the relatively unwieldy carrier, to carry supplies and perform other support services, and to provide additional offensive capabilities. The resulting group of ships is often termed a battle group, carrier group, <a href="/wiki/Carrier_battle_group" title="Carrier battle group">carrier battle group</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carrier_strike_group" title="Carrier strike group">carrier strike group</a>.\n</p><p>There is a view among some military pundits that modern anti-ship weapons systems, such as torpedoes and missiles, or even ballistic missiles with nuclear warheads have made aircraft carriers and carrier groups obsolete as too vulnerable for modern combat.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, the threatening role of aircraft carriers has a place in modern <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_warfare" title="Asymmetric warfare">asymmetric warfare</a>, like the <a href="/wiki/Gunboat_diplomacy" title="Gunboat diplomacy">gunboat diplomacy</a> of the past.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, aircraft carriers facilitate quick and precise projections of overwhelming military power into such local and regional conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg/220px-USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg/330px-USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg/440px-USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="755" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Roosevelt_CV-42_Med_1976-77.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>STOVL Harriers preparing to takeoff from CATOBAR carrier, <a href="/wiki/USS_Franklin_D._Roosevelt_(CV-42)" title="USS Franklin D. Roosevelt (CV-42)">USS&#160;<i>Franklin D. Roosevelt</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>Carriers are large and long ships, although there is a high degree of variation depending on their intended role and <a href="/wiki/Air_wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Air wing">aircraft complement</a>. The size of the carrier has varied over history and among <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">navies</a>, to cater to the various roles that global climates have demanded from <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">naval aviation</a>.\n</p><p>Regardless of size, the ship itself must house their complement of aircraft, with space for launching, storing, and maintaining them. Space is also required for the large crew, supplies (food, munitions, fuel, engineering parts), and propulsion. US aircraft carriers are notable for having <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> powering their systems and propulsion. This makes the carrier reasonably tall.\n</p><p>The top of the carrier is the flight deck, where aircraft are launched and recovered. On the starboard side of this is the island, where air-traffic control and the <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(nautical)" title="Bridge (nautical)">bridge</a> are located.\n</p><p>The constraints of constructing a flight deck affect the role of a given carrier strongly, as they influence the weight, type, and configuration of the aircraft that may be launched. For example, assisted launch mechanisms are used primarily for heavy aircraft, especially those loaded with air-to-ground weapons. CATOBAR is most commonly used on USN fleet carriers as it allows the deployment of heavy jets with full loadouts, especially on ground-attack missions. STOVL is used by other navies because it is cheaper to operate and still provides good deployment capability for <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg/220px-DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg" width="220" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg/330px-DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg/440px-DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg 2x" data-file-width="837" data-file-height="353" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DeHavilland_Vampire_HMS_Ocean_Dec1945_NAN1_47.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first carrier landing and take-off of a jet aircraft: <a href="/wiki/Eric_%22Winkle%22_Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="Eric &quot;Winkle&quot; Brown">Eric "Winkle" Brown</a> landing on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ocean_(R68)" title="HMS Ocean (R68)">HMS&#160;<i>Ocean</i></a> in 1945.</div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the busy nature of the flight deck, only 20 or so aircraft may be on it at any one time. A hangar storage several decks below the flight deck is where most aircraft are kept, and aircraft are taken from the lower storage decks to the flight deck through the use of an elevator. The hangar is usually quite large and can take up several decks of vertical space.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Munitions are commonly stored on the lower decks because they are highly explosive. Usually this is below the water line so that the area can be flooded in case of emergency.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_deck">Flight deck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Flight deck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">Flight deck</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_(CVN_76).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg/220px-US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg/330px-US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg/440px-US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN_76%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_081124-N-3659B-305_F-A-18C_Hornets_launch_from_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Ronald_Reagan_(CVN_76).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Catapult" title="Catapult">Catapult</a> launches aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Ronald_Reagan" title="USS Ronald Reagan">USS&#160;<i>Ronald Reagan</i></a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vikramaditya_5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Vikramaditya_5.jpg/220px-Vikramaditya_5.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Vikramaditya_5.jpg/330px-Vikramaditya_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Vikramaditya_5.jpg/440px-Vikramaditya_5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vikramaditya_5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansive Flight Deck of <a href="/wiki/INS_Vikramaditya" title="INS Vikramaditya">INS&#160;<i>Vikramaditya</i></a> at night showing the <a href="/wiki/Flight_Deck#Ski-jump_ramp" class="mw-redirect" title="Flight Deck">ski-jump ramp</a> used for <a href="/wiki/STOBAR" title="STOBAR">STOBAR</a> configuration</div></div></div>\n<p>As "runways at sea", aircraft carriers have a flat-top <a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">flight deck</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Takeoff" title="Takeoff">launches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">recovers</a> aircraft. Aircraft launch forward, into the wind, and are recovered from astern. The flight deck is where the most notable differences between a carrier and a land runway are found. Creating such a surface at sea poses constraints on the carrier. For example, the fact that it is a ship means that a full-length runway would be costly to construct and maintain. This affects take-off procedure, as a shorter runway length of the deck requires that aircraft <a href="/wiki/Accelerate" class="mw-redirect" title="Accelerate">accelerate</a> more quickly to gain lift. This either requires a thrust boost, a vertical component to its velocity, or a reduced take-off load (to lower mass). The differing types of deck configuration, as above, influence the structure of the flight deck. The form of launch assistance a carrier provides is strongly related to the types of aircraft embarked and the design of the carrier itself.\n</p><p>There are two main philosophies in order to keep the deck short: add thrust to the aircraft, such as using a Catapult Assisted Take-Off (CATO-); and changing the direction of the airplanes\' thrust, as in Vertical and/or Short Take-Off (V/STO-). Each method has advantages and disadvantages of its own:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/CATOBAR" title="CATOBAR">Catapult assisted take-off but arrested recovery</a> (CATOBAR): A steam- or electric-powered <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_catapult" title="Aircraft catapult">catapult</a> is connected to the aircraft, and is used to accelerate conventional aircraft to a safe flying speed. By the end of the catapult stroke, the aircraft is airborne and further propulsion is provided by its own engines. This is the most expensive method as it requires complex machinery to be installed under the flight deck, but allows for even heavily loaded aircraft to take off.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/STOBAR" title="STOBAR">Short take-off but arrested recovery</a> (STOBAR) depends on increasing the net lift on the aircraft. Aircraft do not require catapult assistance for take off; instead on nearly all ships of this type an upwards vector is provided by a ski-jump at the forward end of the flight deck, often combined with thrust vectoring by the aircraft. Alternatively, by reducing the fuel and weapon load, an aircraft is able to reach faster speeds and generate more upwards lift and launch without a ski-jump or catapult.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/STOVL" title="STOVL">Short take-off vertical-landing</a> (STOVL): On aircraft carriers, non-catapult-assisted, fixed-wing short takeoffs are accomplished with the use of <a href="/wiki/Thrust_vectoring" title="Thrust vectoring">thrust vectoring</a>, which may also be used in conjunction with a runway "ski-jump". Use of STOVL tends to allow aircraft to carry a larger payload as compared to during VTOL use, while still only requiring a short runway. The most famous examples are the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Harrier" title="Hawker Siddeley Harrier">Hawker Siddeley Harrier</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Harrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Harrier">Sea Harrier</a>. Although technically VTOL aircraft, they are operationally STOVL aircraft due to the extra weight carried at take-off for fuel and armaments. The same is true of the <a href="/wiki/F-35B_Lightning_II" class="mw-redirect" title="F-35B Lightning II">F-35B Lightning II</a>, which demonstrated VTOL capability in test flights but is operationally STOVL.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/VTOL" title="VTOL">Vertical take-off and landing</a> (VTOL): Aircraft are specifically designed for the purpose of using very high degrees of thrust vectoring (e.g. if the thrust to weight-force ratio is greater than 1, it can take off vertically), but are usually slower than conventionally propelled aircraft.</li></ul>\n<p>On the recovery side of the flight deck, the adaptation to the aircraft loadout is mirrored. Non-VTOL or conventional aircraft cannot decelerate on their own, and almost all carriers using them must have arrested-recovery systems (-BAR, e.g. CATO<i>BAR</i> or STO<i>BAR</i>) to recover their aircraft. Aircraft that are landing extend a <a href="/wiki/Tailhook" title="Tailhook">tailhook</a> that catches on <a href="/wiki/Arrestor_wires" class="mw-redirect" title="Arrestor wires">arrestor wires</a> stretched across the deck to bring themselves to a stop in a short distance. Post-WWII Royal Navy research on safer CATOBAR recovery eventually led to universal adoption of a landing area angled off axis to allow aircraft who missed the arresting wires to "bolt" and safely return to flight for another landing attempt rather than crashing into aircraft on the forward deck.\n</p><p>If the aircraft are VTOL-capable or helicopters, they do not need to decelerate and hence there is no such need. The arrested-recovery system has used an angled deck since the 1950s because, in case the aircraft does not catch the arresting wire, the short deck allows easier take off by reducing the number of objects between the aircraft and the end of the runway. It also has the advantage of separating the recovery operation area from the launch area. <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">Helicopters</a> and aircraft capable of vertical or short take-off and landing (<a href="/wiki/V/STOL" title="V/STOL">V/STOL</a>) usually recover by coming abreast of the carrier on the port side and then using their hover capability to move over the flight deck and land vertically without the need for arresting gear.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Staff_and_deck_operations">Staff and deck operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aircraft_carrier&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Staff and deck operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot;
